- [WALL·E](https://desprookjeswinkel.be/collections/wall-e): Seven hundred years alone on a ruined Earth, compacting the wreckage of human excess into neat little cubes — and still finding beauty in a rubber duck, a Rubik's cube, a single plant pushing through the concrete. Pixar's WALL·E arrived in 2008 and delivered something almost unprecedented in mainstream animation: a film that spent its first forty minutes in near-silence, trusting a small, battered waste-collecting robot to carry the entire emotional weight of the story without a word of dialogue. He managed it completely. WALL·E — Waste Allocation Load Lifter: Earth Class — is one of cinema's great romantic heroes: curious, gentle, relentlessly hopeful, and absolutely convinced that a hand worth holding makes everything worthwhile. His love for EVE, and hers for him, is as tender and true as any love story the medium has produced. WALL·E is also, beneath its extraordinary warmth, one of the sharpest environmental fables in popular culture — a film that looked at consumerism, ecological collapse and human disconnection and made its argument not through lectures but through the image of one small robot doing his job long after everyone else had given up. It landed quietly and has never stopped resonating. Our WALL·E collection brings together officially licensed figures, accessories, homeware and gifts celebrating one of Pixar's most beloved and visually distinctive creations — from WALL·E and EVE Funko Pops and collector's pieces to mugs, prints and keepsakes for fans of the little robot who kept going anyway. - [Lisa Parker](https://desprookjeswinkel.be/collections/lisa-parker): A black cat in firelight. An owl on a moonlit branch. A witch among her herbs and her secrets, entirely comfortable with both. Lisa Parker's world is one you recognise the moment you enter it — and find it very difficult to leave. The British artist has built one of the most distinctive and devoted followings in fantasy gift illustration through a body of work that draws deeply on the aesthetic of folklore, paganism and the natural world — rendered in rich, jewel-dark colour with a technical precision that makes every feather, every whisker, every curl of smoke exactly what it should be. Her cats are particular icons: watchful, luminous, positioned at the meeting point between the domestic and the magical in a way that any cat owner will recognise as entirely accurate. Her owls, ravens, wolves and witches carry the same quality — characters rather than images, each one with an interior life you find yourself imagining. Parker's work sits in a tradition of British gothic illustration that understands the natural world and the supernatural world as continuous rather than separate — the same tradition that runs from Arthur Rackham through Brian Froud and into the present day. It is beautiful work. It is also, unmistakably, work made by someone who means it. Our Lisa Parker collection brings together officially licensed gifts, homeware, accessories and collector's pieces featuring her iconic artwork — from mugs, bags and candle holders to jewellery, figurines and prints for fans of gothic fantasy art at its most atmospheric. For those who light a candle and feel it changes the room. For anyone who has always suspected the cat knows considerably more than it is letting on. The magic is in the details. It always is.

- [National Geographic](https://desprookjeswinkel.be/collections/national-geographic): The world is extraordinary. National Geographic has been proving it since 1888. The yellow border is one of the most recognisable design elements in publishing history — a frame around photographs and stories that have consistently done what the best journalism does: made the distant feel immediate, the unknown feel familiar and the familiar feel newly astonishing. National Geographic was founded by the National Geographic Society in Washington D.C. in 1888 with the mission of increasing and diffusing geographic knowledge, which turned out to be a considerably more ambitious undertaking than it sounded. Over a hundred and thirty years of expeditions, discoveries, wildlife photography, cultural documentation and scientific reportage later, the yellow border has accompanied the world's readers through every corner of the planet and several corners of space. The photography alone would secure its legacy: images that stopped the world, from the Afghan girl whose eyes have been on the cover since 1985 to the wildlife portraits and landscape photographs that have defined how a generation pictures the natural world. But National Geographic has always been more than its images — it is a tradition of curiosity, rigorous reporting and the conviction that knowing more about the world is an unambiguously good thing. The planet is remarkable. The documentation has always risen to meet it. Our National Geographic collection brings together officially licensed gifts, accessories, homeware and educational products inspired by the world's most iconic nature and science brand — for the explorer, the naturalist, the curious mind and the reader who still believes that the world, properly attended to, never stops being surprising. The world is out there. Start here.

A ticket stub, a photograph, a pressed flower, a handwritten note, a strip of washi tape in exactly the right colour. Separately, these are small things. On a page, arranged with intention, they become a record of something that mattered — a holiday, a friendship, a year, a fandom experienced across a decade of books and films and conventions and moments that deserved to be kept properly rather than left to a phone's camera roll. - [Outlander](https://desprookjeswinkel.be/collections/outlander): Scotland, 1743. A stone circle on a highland hill. And a woman who has no business being there — in more ways than one. Diana Gabaldon published the first Outlander novel in 1991 with the reasonable expectation that it would be a single book and the rather less reasonable expectation that it would be easy to categorise. It is a romance, an adventure, a historical novel, a time travel story and an unflinching examination of power, survival and devotion across two centuries — sometimes all within the same chapter. Claire Fraser is one of fiction's great protagonists: a World War Two combat nurse, a time traveller, a healer, a woman of extraordinary pragmatism and extraordinary passion who finds herself in eighteenth-century Scotland and proceeds to navigate it with the combination of  expertise, sharp wit and sheer determined competence that defines everything she does. Jamie Fraser, the man she finds there, is why the fandom has never quite recovered its composure. Starz's television adaptation, which ran from 2014 across eight celebrated seasons, brought the Scottish Highlands, the Jacobite rising and the Fraser marriage to a global audience with production values and an emotional commitment that matched the source material's intensity. The landscapes alone justified the watch. Sam Heughan and Caitriona Balfe justified everything else. Our Outlander collection brings together officially licensed accessories, gifts and keepsakes for fans of Gabaldon's world — for the reader who has been through the books more than once, for the viewer who found the show first, and for anyone who has looked at a stone circle since and felt something unexplainable. Dinna fash. The right gift is here.

Michael Crichton's Jurassic Park arrived as a novel in 1990 and as a Spielberg film in 1993, and both did the same thing: made dinosaurs feel real in a way that nothing had managed before. Not as fossils, not as illustrations in a children's encyclopaedia, but as living, breathing, terrifyingly intelligent animals — and then asked what would happen if someone was arrogant enough to bring them back. The answer, it turned out, was exactly what Dr Malcolm predicted. Life found a way. It usually does. And the T-Rex roar that shook cinema audiences in 1993 has never entirely stopped echoing. Decades on, the franchise has expanded into Jurassic World and beyond, but the original remains untouchable: the amber, the DNA, the glass of water with the ripple, the moment the long neck emerges above the trees and the John Williams score swells and everyone in the cinema quietly forgets to breathe.
